<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A chromosome-anchored eggplant genome sequence reveals key events in Solanaceae evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2019 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-08-13"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="With approximately 450 species, spiny Solanum species constitute the largest monophyletic group in the Solanaceae family, but a high-quality genome assembly from this group is presently missing. We obtained a chromosome-anchored genome assembly of eggplant (Solanum melongena), containing 34,916 genes, confirming that the diploid gene number in the Solanaceae is around 35,000. Comparative genomic studies with tomato (S. lycopersicum), potato (S. tuberosum) and pepper (Capsicum annuum) highlighted the rapid evolution of miRNA:mRNA regulatory pairs and R-type defense genes in the Solanaceae, and provided a genomic basis for the lack of steroidal glycoalkaloid compounds in the Capsicum genus. Using parsimony methods, we reconstructed the putative chromosomal complements of the key founders of the main Solanaceae clades and the rearrangements that led to the karyotypes of extant species and their ancestors. From 10% to 15% of the genes present in the four genomes were syntenic paralogs (ohnologs) generated by the pre-&#947;, &#947; and T paleopolyploidy events, and were enriched in transcription factors. Our data suggest that the basic gene network controlling fruit ripening is conserved in different Solanaceae clades, and that climacteric fruit ripening involves a differential regulation of relatively few components of this network, including CNR and ethylene biosynthetic genes."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>With approximately 450 species, spiny <i>Solanum</i> species constitute the largest monophyletic group in the Solanaceae family, but a high-quality genome assembly from this group is presently missing. We obtained a chromosome-anchored genome assembly of eggplant (<i>Solanum melongena</i>), containing 34,916 genes, confirming that the diploid gene number in the Solanaceae is around 35,000. Comparative genomic studies with tomato (<i>S</i>. <i>lycopersicum</i>), potato (<i>S</i>. <i>tuberosum</i>) and pepper (<i>Capsicum annuum</i>) highlighted the rapid evolution of miRNA:mRNA regulatory pairs and R-type defense genes in the Solanaceae, and provided a genomic basis for the lack of steroidal glycoalkaloid compounds in the <i>Capsicum</i> genus. Using parsimony methods, we reconstructed the putative chromosomal complements of the key founders of the main Solanaceae clades and the rearrangements that led to the karyotypes of extant species and their ancestors. From 10% to 15% of the genes present in the four genomes were syntenic paralogs (ohnologs) generated by the pre-Î³, Î³ and T paleopolyploidy events, and were enriched in transcription factors. Our data suggest that the basic gene network controlling fruit ripening is conserved in different Solanaceae clades, and that climacteric fruit ripening involves a differential regulation of relatively few components of this network, including <i>CNR</i> and ethylene biosynthetic genes.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Solanaceae family comprises around 2,700 plant species, adapted to vastly different environments, and grown for food (tomato, potato, eggplant, pepper), medicinal or recreational uses (belladonna, corkwood tree, mandrake, tobacco) as well as ornamentals (petunia and <i>Brunfelsia</i>). The âgiantâ genus <i>Solanum</i> includes around 1,500 species, among which three staple crops: tomato (<i>S</i>. <i>lycopersicum</i>), potato (<i>S</i>. <i>tuberosum</i>) and eggplant (<i>S</i>. <i>melongena</i>). Unlike tomato and potato, eggplant is native to the Old World, evolved from <i>S</i>. <i>insanum</i> and was independently domesticated in the Indian subcontinent and in China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vavilov, N. The origin, variation, immunity and breeding of cultivated plants. English Transl. by K.S. Chester. Chron. Bot. 13, 1â366 (1951)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d25187e889">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Knapp, S., Vorontsova, M. S. &amp; Prohens, J. Wild relatives of the eggplant (Solanum melongena L.: Solanaceae): new understanding of species names in a complex group. PLoS One 8, e57039 (2013)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d25187e889_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cericola, F. et al. The population structure and diversity of eggplant from Asia and the Mediterranean basin. PLoS One 8, e73702 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d25187e892">3</a></sup>. Eggplant is a representative of the subgenus <i>Leptostemonum</i> (spiny <i>Solanum</i> species), which with around 450 species is the largest monophyletic group in the whole family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="SÃ¤rkinen, T., Bohs, L., Olmstead, R. G. &amp; Knapp, S. A phylogenetic framework for evolutionary study of the nightshades (Solanaceae): a dated 1000-tip tree. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 214 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d25187e903">4</a></sup>. Unlike tomato and similar to pepper, eggplant fruits display ethylene-independent ripening. Several Solanaceae chromosome-anchored genome sequences have been generated to this date, including the ones of potato, tomato and pepper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Consortium, T. P. G. S. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d25187e907">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="The Tomato genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â41 (2012)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d25187e907_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270â8 (2014)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d25187e907_2">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qin, C. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of cultivated and wild peppers provides insights into Capsicum domestication and specialization. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 111, 5135â5140 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d25187e910">8</a></sup>, but a chromosome-anchored reference genome from the <i>Leptostemonum</i> subgenus is presently lacking. A highly fragmented, non chromosome-anchored draft sequence of eggplant is available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Draft genome sequence of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.): the representative solanum species indigenous to the old world. DNA Res. 21, 649â60 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d25187e917">9</a></sup> with an N50 of 64 Kb and 85,446 predicted genes, a number much larger than the approximately 35,000 genes annotated in the other sequenced diploid Solanaceae genomes (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Tab1">1</a>). In this paper, we describe a reference, chromosome-anchored genome sequence of eggplant, and its use for comparative genomics studies with tomato, potato and pepper.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation metrics of the eggplant genome and its comparison with other high-quality Solanaceae genomes and the previous eggplant draft.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly, anchoring and annotation</h3><p>We sequenced and assembled the genome of the inbred eggplant line â67/3â, the male parent of a F6 Recombinant Inbred Line (RIL) population, using a combination of Illumina sequencing and single molecule optical mapping (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">1.1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">1.5.3</a>). A 1.16âGb draft, with an N50 of 0.68âMb, was obtained from Illumina paired end and mate pair libraries assembled using Soapdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1349">10</a></sup>, while the optical map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771â776 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1353">11</a></sup> covered 1.18âGb, with an N50 of 2.56âMb. Their hybrid assembly included 0.92âGb (ungapped) and 1.22âGb (gapped) sequence in 469 scaffolds with an N50 of 3.59âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S3</a>). We estimated the eggplant haploid genome size at 1.21âGb (by flow cytometry) and 1.04âGb (by k-mer distribution) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">S1</a>). The latter is probably an underestimate of the real genome size, as suggested by the presence of secondary peaks in the distribution due to repeats (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">S1</a>).</p><p>Using the SoiLoCo pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1376">12</a></sup> and a linkage map comprising 5,964 markers, developed from an F6 Recombinant Inbred Line (RIL) mapping population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Barchi, L. et al. A RAD tag derived marker based eggplant linkage map and the location of QTLs determining anthocyanin pigmentation. PLoS One 7, e43740 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1380">13</a></sup>, we anchored the hybrid scaffolds to chromosomes (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">1.5.4</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">15.5</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig1">1</a>). Anchored pseudomolecules comprised 1.14âGb (gapped) and 0.82Â Gb (ungapped) genome sequences. The quality of the new assembly is comparable to the ones of tomato, potato and pepper, and significantly improved the metrics of the previous eggplant draft (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Tab1">1</a>). A sequence assembly of line â305E40â, the female parent of the mapping population, was also obtained, with a total size of 1.09âGb and an N50 of 6.9 Kb. Residual heterozygosity was estimated at 0.027% for â67/3â and 0.067% for â305E40â (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">1.6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="661" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Topography of the eggplant genome. Track (<b>A</b>) Eggplant chromosomes with the genetic map (black bars represent the position of genetic markers used for anchoring scaffolds to pseudomolecules; Track (<b>B</b>) gene density; Track (<b>C</b>) RNA-seq expression data density; Track (<b>D</b>) overall repeat density; Track (<b>E</b>) DNA transposon density; Track (<b>F</b>) LTR-Gypsy transposon density; Track (<b>G</b>) LTR-Copia transposon density; Track (<b>H</b>) Intragenome syntenic regions originated from paleopolyploid events (for the parameters used to define syntenic thresholds, see Supplementary Information 2.7). Densities for tracks (<b>B</b>,<b>D</b>,<b>E</b>,<b>F</b>,<b>G</b>) are presented in 1-Mb intervals; for track (<b>C</b>) in 100-Kb intervals).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We annotated the â67/3â assembly with the Maker pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513â24 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1465">14</a></sup> and RNA-Seq data from 19 tissues of â67/3â, obtaining 34,916 high-quality protein-coding gene models (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.3</a>). This number is comparable to the one of other sequenced Solanaceae genomes and lower than the one previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Draft genome sequence of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.): the representative solanum species indigenous to the old world. DNA Res. 21, 649â60 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1472">9</a></sup> (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Tab1">1</a>). Quality controls, based on several pipelines, confirmed that the annotation quality is comparable to that of tomato, potato and pepper (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.3.3</a>). In particular, the annotation comprised 96.9% of Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics btv351 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1486">15</a></sup> (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">S3</a>). About 97% of the annotated â67/3â CDSs were found also in the â305E40â assembly. The genomic landscape of the 12 eggplant chromosomes, similarly to other Solanaceae, shows gene-rich distal chromosome arms and gene-poor peri-centromeric heterochromatin (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig1">1</a>). Based on whole genome data, we estimate the divergence of eggplant from tomato/potato at 15 mya, and from pepper at 20 mya (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.7</a>). OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â89 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1503">16</a></sup> analysis (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.4</a>) showed that 667 gene families are exclusively found in the eggplant lineage with respect to other eudicot lineages (tomato, potato, pepper and Arabidopsis, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">S4</a>). The most common annotation in these families is âpentatricopeptide repeat-containing proteinâ a family of proteins binding to organellar transcripts and modulating organellar gene expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Barkan, A. &amp; Small, I. Pentatricopeptide repeat proteins in plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 415â442 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1513">17</a></sup> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S18</a>). The eggplant genome contains &gt;800 genes encoding pentatricopeptide repeat proteins, about twice the number found in the other four genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Solanaceae genome dynamics</h3><p>Although similar number of genes were found in the four genomes, the eggplant and pepper genome sizes are respectively â1.3-fold and â3.5 -fold larger than those of tomato and potato. Genome expansion of pepper was mainly attributed to a transposition burst of Gypsy LTR retrotransposons, whose dating varies from â13 mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270â8 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1528">7</a></sup> to â0.3 mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qin, C. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of cultivated and wild peppers provides insights into Capsicum domestication and specialization. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 111, 5135â5140 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1532">8</a></sup>. We re-evaluated the transposon abundance and dating in the four Solanaceae genomes (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.2</a>) and confirmed that larger genomes tend to be enriched in Gypsy, followed by Copia elements. The timing of the main burst of LTR transposition is â3 mya for pepper, â2 mya for tomato and potato, and â0.3 mya for eggplant (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig2">2A</a>), furthermore we confirmed the presence of multiple retrotransposition bursts as observed in both monocotyledons and dicotyledons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1542">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Giordani, T., Cavallini, A. &amp; Natali, L. The repetitive component of the sunflower genome. Current Plant Biology 1, 45â54 (2014)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1545">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1545_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707â713 (2014)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1545_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Matsumoto, T. et al. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1545_3">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vitte, C., Panaud, O. &amp; Quesneville, H. LTR retrotransposons in rice (Oryza sativa, L.): recent burst amplifications followed by rapid DNA loss. BMC Genomics 8, 218 (2007)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1545_4">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="DâHont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213â217 (2012)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1545_5">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59â66 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1548">24</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="710" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Solanaceae genome dynamics. (<b>A</b>) Dating of insertions for complete LTR retrotransposons in the various Solanaceae genomes. (<b>B</b>) Syntenic relationships between the chromosomes eggplant (E), tomato (T) and pepper (P). For the parameters used to define synthenic thresholds, see Supplementary Information 2.7. (<b>C</b>) Reconstruction of chromosome dynamics during Solanaceae evolution. Each chromosome of the hypothetical Solanaceae common ancestor is assigned a different colour. The reconstructed karyotypes of evolutionary intermediates are shown on the left, the actual karyotypes of extant species on the right. Chromosomal segments derived from each ancestral chromosome are painted with the corresponding colour, and the arrows indicate the orientation with respect to the ancestral chromosome segment. The minimum number of translocations and inversions occurring between two karyotypes are reported.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Like tomato and potato, both the eggplant and pepper genomes carry signs of the âTâ whole genome triplication first described in the tomato genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="The Tomato genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â41 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1583">6</a></sup>, which we dated at 45 to 55 mya (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.7</a>). The âTâ triplication occurred in the common ancestor of all Solanaceae, as confirmed by the petunia genome sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bombarely, A. et al. Insight into the evolution of the Solanaceae from the parental genomes of Petunia hybrida. Nat. Plants 2, 16074 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1590">25</a></sup>. A set of 3,234 eggplant, 5,099 tomato, 4,659 and 2,163 pepper ohnologs (paralogous genes generated by whole genome polyploidization) are still recognizable in the four genomes. Only 478 ohnologs share the same orthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â89 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1594">16</a></sup> group in the four species, suggesting that genome fractionation following the âTâ triplication was lineage-specific. Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analyses indicated that genes encoding transcription factors are selectively enriched in the extant ohnologs of eggplant, tomato, potato and <i>Capsicum</i> (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.7</a>; Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S27</a>).</p><p>During evolution, the members of the Solanaceae family underwent inter- and intra-chromosomal translocations and inversions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="The Tomato genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â41 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1611">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Wu, F. &amp; Tanksley, S. D. Chromosomal evolution in the plant family Solanaceae. BMC Genomics 11, 182 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1614">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Rinaldi, R. et al. New insights on eggplant/tomato/pepper synteny and identification of eggplant and pepper orthologous QTL. Frontiers in Plant Science 7 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1617">27</a></sup>, which are reflected in the synteny of the extant eggplant, tomato and pepper genomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig2">2B</a>). We used these genomes, plus that of potato and of the outgroup coffee<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181â4 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1624">28</a></sup> to reconstruct chromosomal dynamics during Solanaceae evolution (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.7</a>; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig2">2C</a>). Using parsimony analysis, we first reconstructed the ancestral genomes of the common Solanaceae, <i>Solanum</i> and <i>Potatoe</i> ancestors, and deduced the chromosomal rearrangements leading to the extant genomes in respect to their direct ancestor. <i>Capsicum</i> experienced the highest number of translocations and inversions (54 and 71, respectively), followed by eggplant (18 and 50), while the lowest number, with respect to the common ancestor <i>Potatoe</i>, was detected in potato (3 and 42) and tomato (2 and 21). Several lineage-specific rearrangements were identified: e.g. the translocation of A1 to chromosome (CH) 8 occurred one time both in the pepper and <i>Potatoe</i> lineages, so that pepper, tomato and potato CH1 carry fragments of the ancestral A1 and A8 chromosomes, while eggplant does not. Eggplant CH11 also carries a translocation between A4 and A11, not found in the other three genomes. By computing the frequencies of chromosomal inversions (2.87~5.7 per million years) and translocations (0.27~2.7 per million years), pepper and eggplant showed the highest translocation frequencies (2.7 and 1.22/million years, respectively), much higher than in previous estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Wu, F. &amp; Tanksley, S. D. Chromosomal evolution in the plant family Solanaceae. BMC Genomics 11, 182 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1650">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Rinaldi, R. et al. New insights on eggplant/tomato/pepper synteny and identification of eggplant and pepper orthologous QTL. Frontiers in Plant Science 7 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1653">27</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">miRNA-based gene regulation</h3><p>Using the MIReNA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Mathelier, A. &amp; Carbone, A. MIReNA: finding microRNAs with high accuracy and no learning at genome scale and from deep sequencing data. Bioinformatics 26, 2226â34 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1665">29</a></sup>, we identified 158 high confidence miRNAs belonging to 42 families (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">2.5</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S20</a>), of which 19 families are conserved in many taxonomic groups, while 3 (miR1919, miR5745 and miR6020) are mainly present in Solanaceae (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S21</a>). Putative miRNA targets identified using Tapir<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Bonnet, E., He, Y., Billiau, K. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. TAPIR, a web server for the prediction of plant microRNA targets, including target mimics. Bioinformatics 26, 1566â8 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1678">30</a></sup> in eggplant, resulted in the formation of 1,445 miRNA:mRNA duplex between 146 miRNA and 992 genes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S22</a>). The miRNA families targeting the highest number of genes were 172 and 156, targeting 483 and 144 genes respectively.</p><p>We then zoomed into the function of miR156/157, which belongs to a highly conserved regulatory module in angiosperms, involving <i>SQUAMOSA PROMOTER BINDING</i> (<i>SPB</i>) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wang, H. &amp; Wang, H. The miR156/SPL module, a regulatory hub and versatile toolbox, gears up crops for enhanced agronomic traits. Mol. Plant 8, 677â688 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1695">31</a></sup>. The miRNAs 156/157 were predicted to target 9 <i>SPB</i> genes in tomato and 6 in eggplant (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S22</a>). Both in eggplant and tomato, the ectopic expression of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> miR156/157 caused early release of apical dominance, delayed vegetative phase change (most evident in eggplant miR156/157 plants displaying a light pigmentation typical of the juvenile phase) and delayed onset of flowering (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig3">3</a>), in agreement with the conserved functions of the miR156/157<i>-SPB</i> module in these processes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wang, H. &amp; Wang, H. The miR156/SPL module, a regulatory hub and versatile toolbox, gears up crops for enhanced agronomic traits. Mol. Plant 8, 677â688 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1715">31</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="834" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>miR156 overexpression affects tomato and eggplant development in similar ways. MiR156 â mediated regulation of vegetative growth in tomato (upper panel) and eggplant (lower panel). Wild type plants (left) and plants overexpressing Arabidopsis miR156a (right) under the CaMV <i>35S</i> promoter.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Regulation of fruit ripening, pigmentation, and cuticle biosynthesis</h3><p>We analyzed the expression pattern of the known tomato ripening regulators in tomato (ethylene-dependent ripening), eggplant and pepper (ethylene-independent ripening, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig4">4A</a>). Most of the transcripts, namely <i>RIPENING INHIBITOR</i> (<i>RIN</i>), <i>NON RIPENING</i> (<i>NOR</i>), <i>APETALA</i>2<i>a</i> (<i>AP2a</i>), <i>FRUITFULL1</i> and <i>2</i> (<i>FUL1-2</i>), displayed similar, ripening-associated expression patterns in all three species. <i>AUXIN RESPONSE FACTOR2</i> (<i>ARF2</i>) was ripening-associated only in pepper and tomato, and <i>COLORLESS NON-RIPENING</i> (<i>CNR</i>) only in tomato.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="612" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Evolution of fruit ripening control in the Solanaceae. (<b>A</b>) Expression (log<sub>2</sub> FPKM) of ripening transcription factor regulators <i>CNR</i>, <i>NOR</i>, <i>RIN</i>, <i>TAGL1</i>, <i>FUL1/2</i>, <i>AP2a</i> and <i>ARF2A</i> in different tissue types (root, leaf and flower) plus different stages of fruit ripening (stage 1, stage 2 and stage 3) of eggplant (upper panel), tomato (middle panel) and pepper (lower panel). (<b>B</b>) Co-expression (R-valueââ¥â0.8) of ripening -related genes with the <i>RIN</i> ripening regulator; ellipses, diamonds and rectangles indicate, respectively, transcription factor-, receptor-, and enzyme-encoding genes; purple, red and green shading indicate, respectively, co-expression with <i>RIN</i> in eggplant, tomato and pepper. Detailed co-expression values are shown in Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S31</a>. (<b>C</b>) Wild-type tomato fruit at two stages of ripening (upper left two panels); wild-type eggplant fruit at two stages of ripening (upper right two panels); and over-expression of the <i>TOMATO AGAMOUS-LIKE 1</i> (<i>TAGL1</i>) gene in tomato fruit at two ripening stages (lower left two panels) and in eggplant fruit at two ripening stages (lower right two panels).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also performed co-expression analysis of the whole complement of tomato, eggplant and pepper transcripts using as a bait <i>RIN</i>, a master regulator of ripening in both climacteric and non-climacteric fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. A MADS-box gene necessary for fruit ripening at the tomato ripening-inhibitor (rin) locus. Science 296, 343â6 (2002)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1868">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Dong, T. et al. A non-climacteric fruit gene CaMADS-RIN regulates fruit ripening and ethylene biosynthesis in climacteric fruit. PLoS One 9, e95559 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1871">33</a></sup> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S31</a>). The genes displaying high co-expression with <i>RIN</i> are shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig4">4B</a>. Genes involved in ethylene perception and signal transduction co-expressed across the three species. The <i>ACC SYNTHASE</i> genes (<i>ACS2</i> and <i>ACS4</i>) co-expressed with <i>RIN</i> only in tomato.</p><p>Light is a known regulator of fruit biochemical composition. Accordingly, <i>LONG HYPOCOTYL 5</i> (<i>HY5</i>) transcription factor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Liu, Y. et al. Manipulation of light signal transduction as a means of modifying fruit nutritional quality in tomato. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 101, 9897â9902 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1906">34</a></sup> was highly co-expressed with <i>RIN</i> in eggplant and pepper (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig4">4B</a>) and, to a lesser extent, in tomato (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM2">S31</a>). In contrast, cryptochrome and phytochrome photoreceptors as well as <i>PHYTOCHROME INTERACTING FACTORS</i> (<i>PIF</i>s) show a more species-specific regulation (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig4">4B</a>). <i>EXPANSIN</i> 1 (<i>EXP1</i>) and <i>POLYGALACTURONASE 2A</i> (<i>PG2A</i>) encode important cell wall-modifying enzymes implicated in tomato fruit softening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Seymour, G. B., Ãstergaard, L., Chapman, N. H., Knapp, S. &amp; Martin, C. Fruit development and ripening. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 64, 219â241 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1942">35</a></sup>. The former showed significant co-expression with <i>RIN</i> only in tomato, while two <i>EXP</i> isoforms were co-expressed in eggplant and none in pepper (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig4">4B</a>). <i>PG2A</i> was co-expressed with <i>RIN</i> in tomato and pepper, but not in eggplant.</p><p>Consistent with the high content in phenolics of ripe eggplant fruits, an isoform of <i>PHENYLALANINE AMMONIA LYASE</i> (<i>PAL3</i>) was highly co-expressed with <i>RIN</i> in eggplant, but not in tomato and pepper (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig4">4B</a>). The <i>PHYTOENE SYNTHASE 1</i> (<i>PSY1</i>), the first dedicated step in carotenoid biosynthesis, showed high levels of co-expression with <i>RIN</i> in tomato and pepper, which are rich in these compounds, but not in eggplant. Lastly, the <i>STAY GREEN 1</i> (<i>SGR1</i>) transcription factor, involved in chlorophyll degradation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Barry, C. S., McQuinn, R. P., Chung, M.-Y., Besuden, A. &amp; Giovannoni, J. J. Amino acid substitutions in homologs of the STAY-GREEN protein are responsible for the green-flesh and chlorophyll retainer mutations of tomato and pepper. Plant Physiol. 147, 179â87 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d25187e1993">36</a></sup>, was highly co-expressed with <i>RIN</i> across the three species, in which active chlorophyll degradation occurs during ripening (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig4">4B</a>).</p><p>Ectopic expression of the tomato <i>TAGL1</i> gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. Fleshy fruit expansion and ripening are regulated by the Tomato SHATTERPROOF gene. TAGL1. Plant Cell 21, 3041â62 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2011">37</a></sup> resulted in sepal inflation as well as other ripening-associated features in tomato and eggplant (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig4">4C</a>). The inflated sepals accumulate species-specific pigments: in tomato, at first chlorophyll and leaf-type carotenoids and then lycopene, while in eggplant at first anthocyanins and then orange chalcone and flavonols. This indicates that in both species, <i>TAGL1</i> likely controls the expression of similar sets of developmental genes, but different sets of pigmentation pathway genes.</p><p>In eggplant, commercially ripe fruits (stage 2) accumulate mainly purple/black anthocyanins, while in physiologically ripe (stage 3) fruits the biosynthesis shifts towards orange-colored flavonoids such as naringenin chalcone (Chappell-Maor <i>et al</i>., unpublished data). Several phenylpropanoid biosynthesis genes contribute to the pigmentation pattern (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">3.4</a>). The biosynthetic shift from anthocyanin to flavonoid pigments, occurring between stages 2 and 3, correlates with the down-regulation of the <i>ANTHOCYANIN1</i> (<i>ANT1</i>) and <i>JOHNANDFRANCESCA13</i> (<i>JAF13</i>) transcription factors and the <i>DIHYDROFLAVONOL 4-REDUCTASE</i> (<i>DFR</i>) structural gene as well as the up-regulation of the <i>MYB</i>12 and <i>FLAVONOL SYNTHASE</i> (FLS) genes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig5">5A</a>). Most genes in the carotenoid pathway showed detectable, albeit low expression in ripening eggplant fruits, while <i>CAROTENOID CLEAVAGE DIOXYNENASE 4</i> (<i>CCD4</i>) was highly expressed throughout eggplant fruit ripening (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig5">5A</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1057" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Control of fruit biochemical composition. (<b>A</b>) Schematic pathways for flavonoid (left) and carotenoid (right) biosynthesis in eggplant fruits. Genes and compounds overexpressed at each stage are shown in large bold character. Regulatory genes are indicated in blue. (<b>B</b>) Schematic cuticle biosynthesis pathway. Shortened gene names are indicated in blue. Genes showing fruit cuticle-enriched expression in both eggplant and tomato are indicated in red.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified orthologs of genes known to be involved in wax and/or cutin biosynthesis, and whose expression was enriched in fruit skin of tomato and eggplant (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">3.5</a>). In several gene families, a single ortholog showed a similar degree of fruit skin enrichment in both species, such as <i>ECERIFERUM 6</i> (<i>CER6</i>), Cytochrome P450 (<i>CYP86A4</i>), <i>GLYCEROL-3-PHOSPHATE ACYLTRANSFERASE 6</i> (<i>GPAT6</i>) and <i>CUTIN SYNTHASE</i> (<i>CUS1</i>) in the cutin pathway, <i>FIDDLEHEAD</i> (<i>FDH</i>) in the wax biosynthesis, and the ABC transporters <i>ATP-BINDING CASSETTE G 11/12</i> (<i>ABCG11/12</i>) in transport to the fruit extracellular domain (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig5">5B</a>). Three transcription factors involved in cuticle formation in tomato and/or <i>Arabidopsis</i>, i.e. <i>CUTIN DEFICIENT 2</i> (<i>CD2</i>), <i>MYB30/96</i> and <i>MIXTA-LIKE</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Yeats, T. H. et al. The identification of cutin synthase: formation of the plant polyester cutin. Nat. Chem. Biol. 8, 609â11 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2151">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lashbrooke, J. et al. The tomato MIXTA-Like Transcription Factor Coordinates Fruit Epidermis Conical Cell Development and Cuticular Lipid Biosynthesis and Assembly. Plant Physiol. 169, 2553â71 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2154">39</a></sup> also showed skin-enriched expression in tomato and eggplant fruits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evolution of pathogen resistance and glycoalkaloid biosynthesis</h3><p>Annotation of main resistance protein classes highlighted significant amplifications in the four analyzed species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Andolfo, G. et al. Overview of tomato (Solanum lycopersicum) candidate pathogen recognition genes reveals important Solanum R locus dynamics. New Phytol. 197, 223â37 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2167">40</a></sup>. The potato and pepper genomes showed a significant amplification of <i>NUCLEOTIDE-BINDING LEUCINE-RICH REPEAT</i> (<i>NB-LRR</i>) genes involved in pathogen defense (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">3.1</a>). Two large expansions of <i>NB-LRR</i> genes, involving respectively the <i>Gpa2/Bs2/Rx/Rx2</i> and <i>Mi1</i>.<i>2/Hero/Rpi-blb2</i> subfamilies, are present in the pepper genome, while five subfamilies, including genes conferring resistance to <i>Phytophthora infestans</i>, are expanded in potato (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig6">6A</a>). Solanaceae genomes evolved preserving highly active R-islands in which the internal variability is regulated in species-specific manner. Species-specific diversification at individual resistance loci was mediated by tandem duplication of distinct founder paralogs in each species, as exemplified by the cluster on CH6, comprising the potato <i>Rpi-blb2</i> gene (resistance to <i>P</i>. <i>infestans</i>), and the tomato <i>Mi1</i>.<i>2</i> gene (nematode resistance) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig6">6B</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-47985-w/MediaObjects/41598_2019_47985_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="576" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Gene family evolution in the Solanaceae. (<b>A</b>) Phylogenetic tree of CNL (in blue) and TNL (in green) <i>NB-LRR</i> genes. The distinctive evolutionary dynamics of ten <i>NB-LRR</i> groups in eggplant (purple), potato (yellow), tomato (red) and pepper (green) genomes are displayed. The number of <i>NB-LRR</i> genes included in each group is indicated on the vertical axis. The plant pathogens (fungi, virus, bacteria or nematodes) to which known genes in the clade confer resistance are indicated by pictures. <i>Gro1</i>.<i>4</i> and <i>Hero</i> confer resistance to <i>Globodera rostochiensis</i>. <i>Gpa2</i> and <i>Mi1</i>.<i>2</i> confer resistance to <i>Globodera pallida</i> and <i>Meloidogyne incognita</i>, respectively. <i>Rx</i> and <i>Rx2</i> confer resistance to <i>Potato Virus X</i>. <i>N</i>, <i>Ry1</i>, <i>Tm2</i> and <i>Sw5</i> confer resistance to Tobacco Mosaic Virus, Potato Virus Y, Tomato Mosaic Virus and Tomato Spotted Wilt Virus, respectively. <i>Bs2</i> and <i>Bs4</i> confer resistance to <i>Xanthomonas campestris</i> and <i>Prf</i> to <i>Pseudomonas syringae</i>. <i>Rpi-blb1</i>, <i>Rpi-blb2</i>, <i>R1</i>, <i>R2</i> and <i>R3</i> confer resistance to <i>Phytophthora infestans</i> and <i>I2</i> to <i>Fusarium oxysporum</i>. (<b>B</b>) Independent expansion of <i>Mi1</i>.<i>2</i>/<i>Rpi-blb2</i> homologous genes in eggplant, pepper, potato and tomato. The human APAF1 (Apoptotic Protease Activating Factor 1), is a human cytoplasmic protein showing similar structure to nucleotide binding siteâleucine rich repeat proteins of plants, and was used to re-root the tree of plant NLR phylogenetic analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Andolfo, G. et al. Overview of tomato (Solanum lycopersicum) candidate pathogen recognition genes reveals important Solanum R locus dynamics. New Phytol. 197, 223â37 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2345">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Lozano, R., Hamblin, M. T., Prochnik, S. &amp; Jannink, J.-L. Identification and distribution of the NBS-LRR gene family in the Cassava genome. BMC Genomics 16, 360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2348">79</a></sup>. (<b>C</b>) The steroidal glycoalkaloid metabolic gene cluster in four Solanaceous genomes. In tomato, six genes (<i>GAME1</i>, <i>GAME2</i>, <i>GAME6</i>, <i>GAME11</i>, <i>GAME17</i>, <i>GAME18</i>) are located on CH7 and two (<i>GAME4</i> and <i>GAME12</i>) on CH12. In potato, four-SGA associated genes (<i>SGT3</i>, <i>GAME6</i>, <i>GAME11</i> and <i>SGT1</i>/<i>GAME1</i>) are found in CH7 and two in CH12 (<i>GAME4</i> and <i>GAME12</i>). In eggplant, four putative SGA genes namely, <i>GAME1</i>/<i>SGT1</i>, <i>SGT3</i>, <i>GAME6</i> and <i>GAME11</i> are also physically linked to each other on CH7 and two, <i>GAME4</i> and <i>GAME12</i> on CH12. In pepper, five genes (<i>GAME1</i>, <i>GAME6</i>, <i>GAME11</i>, <i>GAME17</i> and <i>GAME18</i>) were found to be present on CH7. <i>GAME4</i> and <i>GAME12</i> homologous sequences were not detected in the pepper genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In the tomato and potato genomes, most core genes for Steroidal Glycoalkaloid (SGA) biosynthesis genes form two metabolic gene clusters, on CH7 and CH12<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Itkin, M. et al. Biosynthesis of antinutritional alkaloids in solanaceous crops is mediated by clustered genes. Science 341, 175â9 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2458">41</a></sup> (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">3.2</a>; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig6">6C</a>). The cluster on CH7 was also found in eggplant and pepper, while the one on CH12, which contains <i>GLYCOALKALOID METABOLISM 4</i> and <i>12</i> (<i>GAME4 and GAME12</i>), only in eggplant (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#Fig6">6C</a>). A BLAST search of the pepper genome did not yield any genes closely related to <i>GAME4</i> or <i>GAME12</i>. Since these two genes catalyze, respectively, the first and second step in the conversion of a furostanol-type saponin precursor into SGAs, their absence in pepper is likely responsible for the absence of SGAs in this species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>With around 450 species, the spiny Solanums represent the largest monophyletic group in the Solanaceae family. We obtained a high-quality, anchored eggplant genome sequence that fills an important gap for comparative genomics studies in the Solanaceae. The sequence was obtained through assembly of Illumina reads and further scaffolding/error correction using optical mapping. Gene annotation was assisted by RNA-Seq data from 19 different eggplant tissues/organs and resulted in 34,916 high-quality gene models, similarly to what was observed in other Solanaceae species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Chromosome dynamics and their contribution to Solanaceae genome diversity</h3><p>We found signs of the âTâ triplication in the eggplant and pepper genomes and dated it between 45â55 mya, i.e. slightly more recent than previous estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="The Tomato genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â41 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2503">6</a></sup>. This, and the recent discovery of signs of the âTâ triplication also in petunia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bombarely, A. et al. Insight into the evolution of the Solanaceae from the parental genomes of Petunia hybrida. Nat. Plants 2, 16074 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2507">25</a></sup>, may indicate that Solanaceae radiation is more recent than previously reported. One of the main effects of the âTâ triplication is the generation of paralogous genes, or ohnologs, a fraction of which are still nowadays triplicated or duplicated. In such ohnologs, we detected an enrichment of genes encoding transcription factors. It has been suggested that one of the effects of gene-balanced polyploidizations is to leave behind duplicate âfunctional modulesâ, such as interacting transcription factor groups, which in turn increase morphological complexity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Freeling, M. Bias in Plant Gene Content Following Different Sorts of Duplication: Tandem, Whole-Genome, Segmental, or by Transposition. Annual Review of Plant Biology 60(1), 433â453 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2511">42</a></sup>. This is what we observed in extant Solanaceae genomes, and may explain the extreme morphological variation and ecological adaptability of this plant family.</p><p>In the Solanaceae genomes analyzed, we detected signs of multiple retrotransposition bursts. The main burst in eggplant is the most recent (â0.3 mya) while in pepper is the most ancient (â3 mya). Since the main pepper burst occurred much later than the <i>Solanum</i>-<i>Capsicum</i> divergence (20 mya), our data did not confirm the hypothesis that retrotransposition bursts contributed to the reproductive isolation of different Solanaceae clades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270â8 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2524">7</a></sup>. Using COS markers, 0.1~1 inversions per million years and 0.2~0.4 translocations per million years were estimated in the four lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Wu, F. &amp; Tanksley, S. D. Chromosomal evolution in the plant family Solanaceae. BMC Genomics 11, 182 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2528">26</a></sup>, with the eggplant lineage experiencing an approximately double inversion rate than the other three. The frequencies we calculated, based on the whole genome sequences, were much higher for both translocations (0.27~2.7 per million years) and inversions (2.87~5.7 per million years). This is probably due to the higher resolving power of the high-quality genomes used in our analysis with respect to the COS maps. Compared to the other three Solanaceae, pepper shows a very high rate of putative translocations (2.7/million years), followed by eggplant (1.22/million years). Pepper and eggplant also carry the highest number of retrotransposons, suggesting that chromosomal translocations could have been mediated by recombination between homologous retrotransposons located on different chromosomes, as reported for yeast<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Mieczkowski, P. A., Lemoine, F. J. &amp; Petes, T. D. Recombination between retrotransposons as a source of chromosome rearrangements in the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae. DNA Repair (Amst). 5, 1010â1020 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2532">43</a></sup>.</p><p>An additional mechanism contributing to the functional plasticity of Solanaceae genomes is gene duplication, exemplified by R gene diversification, which occurred at very different rates in different species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Di Donato, A., Andolfo, G., Ferrarini, A., Delledonne, M. &amp; Ercolano, M. R. Investigation of orthologous pathogen recognition gene-rich regions in solanaceous species. Genome 60, 850â859 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2539">44</a></sup>. Tomato and eggplant show relatively low rates of RÂ gene duplication, while potato and pepper show much higher ones. Tandem duplications of R genes are generally lineage-specific, with the majority of events occurring after the separation of the major Solanaceae clades, however our data also highlighted additional tandem duplications which resulted in eggplant-specific gene clusters sharing homology with characterized TNL resistance loci.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Evolution of secondary metabolism</h3><p>In angiosperms, gene clusters encoding enzymes for specialized secondary metabolites mediate the synthesis of defense compounds, such as hydroxamic acid derivatives, alkaloids, cyanogenic glucosides, and SGAs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="NÃ¼tzmann, H.-W. &amp; Osbourn, A. Gene clustering in plant specialized metabolism. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 26, 91â99 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2551">45</a></sup>. Several hypotheses may explain the evolution of these clusters, including co-regulation and/or co-inheritance of clustered genes. SGAs are involved in the defense against herbivores and are produced by numerous members of the <i>Solanum</i> genus, including tomato, potato and eggplant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="CÃ¡rdenas, P. D. et al. The bitter side of the nightshades: Genomics drives discovery in Solanaceae steroidal alkaloid metabolism. Phytochemistry 113, 24â32 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2558">46</a></sup> (Supplementary InformationÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#MOESM1">3.2</a>). In contrast, pepper does not produce glycoalkaloids but steroidal glycosides, saponins and capsaicinoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Andersson, C. Glycoalkaloids in tomatoes, eggplants, pepper and two Solanum species growing wild in the Nordic countries. (TemaNord, 1999)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2565">47</a></sup>. As previously reported in tomato and potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Itkin, M. et al. Biosynthesis of antinutritional alkaloids in solanaceous crops is mediated by clustered genes. Science 341, 175â9 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2570">41</a></sup>, also in eggplant the SGAs biosynthesis genes are clustered on CH7 and CH12 and are co-regulated. However, the CH12 cluster, which encodes the first two dedicated steps in the SGA pathway after the common precursor of SGAs and steroidal saponins, is missing in pepper, suggesting that the gain/loss of this cluster served as an evolutionary switch mediating the rerouting of steroidal metabolism from steroidal saponinsÂ to SGAs and <i>vice versa</i>.</p><p>Pigmentation of fleshy fruits is strongly influenced by coevolution with the frugivorous animals that perform seed dispersal, with red and black fruits prevailing in plants whose seeds are dispersed by birds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Willson, M. F. &amp; Whelan, C. J. The evolution of fruit color in fleshy-fruited plants., &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1086/285132&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2580">48</a></sup>. Our data indicate a similar regulation in tomato, potato and pepper fruits of the <i>STAY GREEN</i> gene, encoding a plastid-localized protein that enhances both chlorophyll degradation and carotenoid biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Barry, C. S., McQuinn, R. P., Chung, M.-Y., Besuden, A. &amp; Giovannoni, J. J. Amino acid substitutions in homologs of the STAY-GREEN protein are responsible for the green-flesh and chlorophyll retainer mutations of tomato and pepper. Plant Physiol. 147, 179â87 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2587">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Giuliano, G. Plant carotenoids: genomics meets multi-gene engineering. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 19, 111â117 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2590">49</a></sup>. This, and the induction in fruits of all three species of a <i>PSY</i> gene which encode the rate-limiting step of carotenoid biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Giuliano, G. Plant carotenoids: genomics meets multi-gene engineering. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 19, 111â117 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-47985-w#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d25187e2597">49</a></sup>, indicates that chlorophyll degradation and carotenoid biosynthesis is regulated in a similar way in the three species during fruit ripening. The lack of carotenoids in eggplant fruits can probably be attributed to high expression of carotenoid-cleaving enzymes such as CCD4, as already described in white peach fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Brandi, F. et al. Study of âRedhavenâ peach and its white-fleshed mutant suggests a key role of CCD4 carotenoid dioxygenase in carotenoid and norisoprenoid volatile metabolism.
